class="infobox-data">28,434 acres (115.07&#160;km<sup>2</sup>)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr class="note"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Established</th><td class="infobox-data">January&#160;17, 1903<span class="noprint">&#59;&#32;121 years ago</span><span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1903-01-17</span>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" scope="row" class="infobox-label">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://www.fs.usda.gov/elyunque/">http://www.fs.usda.gov/elyunque/</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>El Yunque National Forest</b> (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">Bosque Nacional El Yunque</i>), formerly known as the <b>Caribbean National Forest</b> (or <i>Bosque Nacional del Caribe</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a forest located in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>. While there are both temperate and tropical rainforests in other states and territories, it is the only <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Forest" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Forest">United States National Forest</a> System and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">United States Forest Service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> El Yunque National Forest is located on the slopes of the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Luquillo" title="Sierra de Luquillo">Sierra de Luquillo</a> mountains, encompassing more than 28,000 acres (43.753&#160;mi<sup>2</sup> or 113.32&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of land, making it the largest block of <a href="/wiki/Public_land" title="Public land">public land</a> in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>.\n</p><p>The forest contains and is named after named <a href="/wiki/El_Yunque_(Puerto_Rico)" title="El Yunque (Puerto Rico)">Pico El Yunque</a>, the second highest mountain in the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Luquillo" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierra Luquillo">Sierra de Luquillo</a>. Other peaks within the national forest are <a href="/wiki/Pico_del_Este_(Sierra_de_Luquillo)" title="Pico del Este (Sierra de Luquillo)">Pico del Este</a>, Pico del Oeste, El Cacique and the highest peak, <a href="/wiki/El_Toro_(Sierra_de_Luquillo)" title="El Toro (Sierra de Luquillo)">El Toro</a>, which is the highest point in the national forest and in eastern Puerto Rico rising 3,494 feet (1,065 m) above sea level.\n</p><p>Ample rainfall (over 20 feet a year in some areas, or an average of 120 inches of water up to 240 inches of water a year) creates a jungle-like setting\xe2\x80\x94lush foliage, crags, waterfalls, and rivers are a frequent sight. The forest has a number of trails from which the jungle-like territory\'s <a href="/wiki/Flora_and_fauna" class="mw-redirect" title="Flora and fauna">flora and fauna</a> can be appreciated. El Yunque forest is also renowned for its unique <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Ta\xc3\xadno people">Ta\xc3\xadno</a> <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">petroglyphs</a>. It is said that indigenous people believed that El Yunque was the throne of their chief god <a href="/wiki/Y%C3%BAcahu" title="Y\xc3\xbacahu">Y\xc3\xbacahu</a>, so that it is the Caribbean equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olympus" title="Mount Olympus">Mount Olympus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>.\n</p>\n<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Naming_and_etymology">Naming and etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Naming and etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Three-pointed_sculpture_with_carved_face_(zimi),_Taino_Culture,_Puerto_Rico,_c._1000-1494_AD,_stone_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08790.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Three-pointed_sculpture_with_carved_face_%28zimi%29%2C_Taino_Culture%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_c._1000-1494_AD%2C_stone_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08790.JPG/220px-Three-pointed_sculpture_with_carved_face_%28zimi%29%2C_Taino_Culture%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_c._1000-1494_AD%2C_stone_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08790.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="194" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Three-pointed_sculpture_with_carved_face_%28zimi%29%2C_Taino_Culture%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_c._1000-1494_AD%2C_stone_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08790.JPG/330px-Three-pointed_sculpture_with_carved_face_%28zimi%29%2C_Taino_Culture%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_c._1000-1494_AD%2C_stone_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08790.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Three-pointed_sculpture_with_carved_face_%28zimi%29%2C_Taino_Culture%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_c._1000-1494_AD%2C_stone_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08790.JPG/440px-Three-pointed_sculpture_with_carved_face_%28zimi%29%2C_Taino_Culture%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_c._1000-1494_AD%2C_stone_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08790.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3536" data-file-height="3112" /></a><figcaption>The three-pointed <a href="/wiki/Zemi" title="Zemi">zemi</a> is often said to represent <a href="/wiki/Y%C3%BAcahu" title="Y\xc3\xbacahu">Yokahu</a></figcaption></figure>\n<p><i>El Yunque</i> is the name of the mountain peak, <a href="/wiki/El_Yunque_(Puerto_Rico)" title="El Yunque (Puerto Rico)">Pico El Yunque</a>, the name of the forest and, colloquially on some occasions, the name for the entire <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Luquillo" title="Sierra de Luquillo">Sierra de Luquillo</a> range. <i>El Yunque</i> most likely comes from or relates to the Spanish word <i>yunque</i>, meaning <a href="/wiki/Anvil" title="Anvil">anvil</a>. This name references the relatively high and flat shape of the Sierra de Luquillo range when observed from the north (El Yunque Peak) or the south (<a href="/wiki/El_Toro_(Sierra_de_Luquillo)" title="El Toro (Sierra de Luquillo)">El Toro</a>). It is also said that the Spanish name might have been influenced by the native names for the mountain: <i>Yuk\xc3\xa9n</i> or <i>Yuke</i> possibly meaning "white land", and <i>Luquillo</i> or <i>Yukiyu</i>, another name for the spirit or deity <a href="/wiki/Y%C3%BAcahu" title="Y\xc3\xbacahu">Yokahu</a> and also the name of a legendary cacique, <a href="/wiki/Loquillo_(chief)" class="mw-redirect" title="Loquillo (chief)">Loquillo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-El_Yunque:_Its_History_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-El_Yunque:_Its_History-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is not known whether the mountain gives its name to the range or vice versa.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Awesome_view_form_the_Three_Peaks_in_El_Yunque.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Awesome_view_form_the_Three_Peaks_in_El_Yunque.jpg/220px-Awesome_view_form_the_Three_Peaks_in_El_Yunque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Awesome_view_form_the_Three_Peaks_in_El_Yunque.jpg/330px-Awesome_view_form_the_Three_Peaks_in_El_Yunque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Awesome_view_form_the_Three_Peaks_in_El_Yunque.jpg/440px-Awesome_view_form_the_Three_Peaks_in_El_Yunque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>View from the Three Peaks (Tres Picachos) in El Yunque</figcaption></figure><p>\nThe area of the El Yunque National Forest has been notable since the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> era. The forest today is home to several archaeological sites related to the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno" title="Ta\xc3\xadno">Ta\xc3\xadnos</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Icacos_Petroglyph_Group" title="Icacos Petroglyph Group">R\xc3\xado Blanco petroglyphs</a>, although no evidence of permanent settlement has been found in the area, which suggests that it was possibly avoided and most likely considered <a href="/wiki/Sacred_mountains" title="Sacred mountains">sacred</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Radar_towers_atop_a_mountain_at_El_Yunque_rain_forest_-_panoramio.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Radar_towers_atop_a_mountain_at_El_Yunque_rain_forest_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-Radar_towers_atop_a_mountain_at_El_Yunque_rain_forest_-_panoramio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Radar_towers_atop_a_mountain_at_El_Yunque_rain_forest_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-Radar_towers_atop_a_mountain_at_El_Yunque_rain_forest_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Radar_towers_atop_a_mountain_at_El_Yunque_rain_forest_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-Radar_towers_atop_a_mountain_at_El_Yunque_rain_forest_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a><figcaption>Radar towers, such as this one in <a href="/wiki/Pico_del_Este_(Sierra_de_Luquillo)" title="Pico del Este (Sierra de Luquillo)">Pico del Este</a>, installed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">US Navy</a> during the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>.</figcaption></figure><p>The Spanish began the conquest the island of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Borinquen</a> in 1493, and gold was soon found in the <a href="/wiki/Fajardo_River" title="Fajardo River">Fajardo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blanco_River_(Naguabo,_Puerto_Rico)" title="Blanco River (Naguabo, Puerto Rico)">Blanco Rivers</a> in 1509, which sparked interest in <a href="/wiki/Gold_mining" title="Gold mining">gold mining</a> in the area. By 1513 there were gold mines in both river areas and gold was soon found in other rivers of the mountain range such as the <a href="/wiki/Sabana_River_(Naguabo,_Puerto_Rico)" title="Sabana River (Naguabo, Puerto Rico)">Sabana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prieto_River_(Naguabo,_Puerto_Rico)" title="Prieto River (Naguabo, Puerto Rico)">Prieto</a>, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_de_la_Mina_(R%C3%ADo_Grande,_Puerto_Rico)" title="R\xc3\xado de la Mina (R\xc3\xado Grande, Puerto Rico)">La Mina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mameyes_River" title="Mameyes River">Mameyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Esp%C3%ADritu_Santo" title="R\xc3\xado Esp\xc3\xadritu Santo">Esp\xc3\xadritu Santo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Can%C3%B3vanas_River" title="Can\xc3\xb3vanas River">Can\xc3\xb3vanas</a> rivers. The period of gold mining in the area however ended in 1530 when miners and settlers started being attacked by rebelling Ta\xc3\xadnos, and the area would not be settled again until the 17th century. The area where El Portal Rainforest Visitor Center is located used to be the site of a coffee plantation named <i>Hacienda Catalina</i>. <a href="/wiki/Coffea_arabica" title="Coffea arabica">Coffee</a> was introduced and cultivated in the slopes of El Yunque in the 1730s but the area remained relatively untouched until the 19th century when the lowland forests were exploited for <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">timber</a> and cut down for the development of agriculture, which attracted the attention of the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Spain" title="Monarchy of Spain">Spanish Crown</a>. The forest during this time was part of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_land" title="Crown land">Crown Lands</a> of Puerto Rico and in 1853 the Spanish government sent foresters from the <i>Inspecci\xc3\xb3n de Montes</i> (the Spanish Forest Service) to survey the land. Due to the rapid population growth in the island and the poor farming practices of the impoverished <a href="/wiki/Royal_Decree_of_Graces_of_1815" title="Royal Decree of Graces of 1815">European immigrants</a> of the time, the forest region was formally set aside in 1876 by King <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_XII_of_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfonso XII of Spain">Alfonso XII of Spain</a> with the purpose of preserving the soil and water resources and regulating the <a href="/wiki/Wood_industry" title="Wood industry">timber industry</a> in the region. This makes El Yunque one of the oldest reserves in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>, only four years younger than <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\nThe Spanish Crown ceded Puerto Rico to the United States in <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War">1898</a>. At the time, most of the original forests in Puerto Rico had been either cut or burned down, and the highlands of the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Luquillo" title="Sierra de Luquillo">Sierra de Luquillo</a> contained the last remaining tracts of <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">untouched forest</a> in the island. President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> set aside the previous Crown Lands of El Yunque area, and the <b>Luquillo Forest Reserve</b> was established on 17 January 1903 by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_General_Land_Office" title="United States General Land Office">United States General Land Office</a> with 65,950 acres (266.9&#160;km<sup>2</sup>). It became a National Forest in 1906 and renamed the <b>Caribbean National Forest</b> on 4 June 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The forest was still used for timber during the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World Wars</a>, and several military installations such as radars and communications infrastructure were installed in the forest, particularly on the highest peaks, such as the early-warning radar site installed in <a href="/wiki/El_Yunque_(Puerto_Rico)" title="El Yunque (Puerto Rico)">Pico El Yunque</a> to observe and protect against possible incursions by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">German</a> aircraft and submarines.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-El_Yunque:_Its_History_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-El_Yunque:_Its_History-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:El_Yunque_National_Forest_(37793583751).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/El_Yunque_National_Forest_%2837793583751%29.jpg/220px-El_Yunque_National_Forest_%2837793583751%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/El_Yunque_National_Forest_%2837793583751%29.jpg/330px-El_Yunque_National_Forest_%2837793583751%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/El_Yunque_National_Forest_%2837793583751%29.jpg/440px-El_Yunque_National_Forest_%2837793583751%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a><figcaption>A view of the forest after <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Maria" title="Hurricane Maria">Hurricane Maria</a>, which hit on September 20, 2017 - the trees were left leafless.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>The Caribbean National Forest was designated an <i>Insular Wildlife Refuge</i> by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">US Department of Agriculture</a> in 1946, and reforestation efforts were also established between 1934 and 1948 to revitalize the parts of the forest that had been formerly lost due to human activity. The <a href="/wiki/Luquillo_Experimental_Forest" title="Luquillo Experimental Forest">Luquillo Experimental Forest</a> was established in 1956 to promote scientific research and expand conservation efforts for the critically endangered <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_amazon" title="Puerto Rican amazon">Puerto Rican parrot</a> and the recently discovered yet endangered <a href="/wiki/Elfin_woods_warbler" title="Elfin woods warbler">elfin woods warbler</a>. Most of the recreational infrastructure was developed in the 1980s and the field offices were moved to their current location in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> established <i>El Verde Field Station</i> in 1988 under an agreement with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">US Forest Service</a> as a primary site for the <i>Luquillo Long-Term Ecological Research</i> (Luquillo-LTER, now managed by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Puerto_Rico,_R%C3%ADo_Piedras_Campus" title="University of Puerto Rico, R\xc3\xado Piedras Campus">University of Puerto Rico, Rio Piedras</a>) with the goal of supporting scientific research of tropical ecosystems due to the occurrence of five out of six of <a href="/wiki/Holdridge_life_zones" title="Holdridge life zones">Holdridge\'s life zones</a> and forest types.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\nThe forest was heavily damaged by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hugo" title="Hurricane Hugo">Hurricane Hugo</a> in 1989 and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Georges" title="Hurricane Georges">Hurricane Georges</a> in 1998, and it is estimated that only 23 individual parrots remained in the wild afterwards. Between 2000 and 2002, 35 captive parrots were released into the wild as part of a program to save the critically endangered bird. An <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive order (United States)">executive order</a> signed by President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> on 2 April 2007 changed the name of the Caribbean National Forest to <b>El Yunque National Forest</b>, better reflecting the cultural and historical feelings of the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Ricans" title="Puerto Ricans">Puerto Rican people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Irma" title="Hurricane Irma">Hurricanes Irma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Maria" title="Hurricane Maria">Maria</a> caused deforestation, landslides and heavy damage to the forest and its infrastructure in 2017, drastically reducing the Puerto Rican parrot population once again.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2020 the population has begun to recover and the captive population\'s reproduction rates has also doubled under the supervision of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fish_and_Wildlife_Service" title="United States Fish and Wildlife Service">Fish and Wildlife Service</a>. The Portal Rainforest Visitor Center reopened at the end of 2021, after being reconstructed following Hurricane Maria. El Yunque National Forest today extends over 28,000 acres through the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Grande,_Puerto_Rico" title="R\xc3\xado Grande, Puerto Rico">R\xc3\xado Grande</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luquillo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Luquillo, Puerto Rico">Luquillo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naguabo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Naguabo, Puerto Rico">Naguabo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ceiba,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ceiba, Puerto Rico">Ceiba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fajardo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Fajardo, Puerto Rico">Fajardo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Can%C3%B3vanas,_Puerto_Rico" title="Can\xc3\xb3vanas, Puerto Rico">Can\xc3\xb3vanas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Las_Piedras,_Puerto_Rico" title="Las Piedras, Puerto Rico">Las Piedras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Juncos,_Puerto_Rico" title="Juncos, Puerto Rico">Juncos</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Climate">Climate</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Climate"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:View_direction_Dos_Picachos_from_El_Pico_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/View_direction_Dos_Picachos_from_El_Pico_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest.JPG/220px-View_direction_Dos_Picachos_from_El_Pico_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/View_direction_Dos_Picachos_from_El_Pico_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest.JPG/330px-View_direction_Dos_Picachos_from_El_Pico_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/View_direction_Dos_Picachos_from_El_Pico_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest.JPG/440px-View_direction_Dos_Picachos_from_El_Pico_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a><figcaption>View from the top of <a href="/wiki/El_Yunque_(Puerto_Rico)" title="El Yunque (Puerto Rico)">Pico El Yunque</a> with exceptional visibility</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Because Puerto Rico is located between the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Capricorn" title="Tropic of Capricorn">Tropic of Capricorn</a>, it has a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical climate</a>, more specifically a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest_climate" title="Tropical rainforest climate">tropical rainforest climate</a>, with higher elevations over 3,000 feet bordering on a <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_highland_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Subtropical highland climate">subtropical highland climate</a>. There is no distinct <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">wet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dry_season" title="Dry season">dry season</a> in El Yunque; it rains year-round. The temperature and length of daylight remain fairly constant throughout the year. The average temperature in the summer is 80&#160;\xc2\xb0F (26&#160;\xc2\xb0C) high and 68&#160;\xc2\xb0F (20&#160;\xc2\xb0C) low and in the winter 72&#160;\xc2\xb0F (22&#160;\xc2\xb0C) high and 58&#160;\xc2\xb0F (15&#160;\xc2\xb0C) low, Temperatures can drop below 50&#160;\xc2\xb0F (10&#160;\xc2\xb0C) on clear nights during the winter, but never below freezing. All of these factors provide a year-round growing season.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Ecology_and_conservation">Ecology and conservation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ecology and conservation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_La_Mina_river_and_trail_in_El_Yunque.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_La_Mina_river_and_trail_in_El_Yunque.jpg/220px-The_La_Mina_river_and_trail_in_El_Yunque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_La_Mina_river_and_trail_in_El_Yunque.jpg/330px-The_La_Mina_river_and_trail_in_El_Yunque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_La_Mina_river_and_trail_in_El_Yunque.jpg/440px-The_La_Mina_river_and_trail_in_El_Yunque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_de_la_Mina_(R%C3%ADo_Grande,_Puerto_Rico)" title="R\xc3\xado de la Mina (R\xc3\xado Grande, Puerto Rico)">La Mina River</a> and trail in El Yunque - leads to La Mina roaring waterfall and connects to the Big Trees Trail.</figcaption></figure><p>Its ecosystem is specifically surveyed by the Management Team of Ecosystems (Equipo de Manejos de Ecosistemas), which is led by Pedro Rios.<sup id="cite_ref-About_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to its location in the northeastern part of Puerto Rico, the incoming <a href="/wiki/Trade_wind" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade wind">trade winds</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> bash into the mountains, leading to an excess of rainfall registered at about 240 inches (6.1&#160;m) per year.<sup id="cite_ref-About_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This process is called <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">orographic lift</a> and accounts for the intense rainfall and constant cloud presence in this mountainous region. This constant cloud cover and persistent winds produced by the <a href="/wiki/Adiabatic_process" title="Adiabatic process">adiabatic process</a> of air particles rushing up through the mountainside has affected the morphology of El Yunque, but the most effect has been on the <i>bosque enano</i> or <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_forest" title="Dwarf forest">dwarf forest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Weaver_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>The forest is home to over 200 species of trees and plants, 16 of which are <a href="/wiki/Endemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endemic">endemic</a> to the forest.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Critically_endangered_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Critically endangered species">critically endangered</a> <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_amazon" title="Puerto Rican amazon">Puerto Rican amazon</a> (<i>Amazona vittata</i>), with an estimated wild population of 58\xe2\x80\x9380 individuals in the wild, occurred exclusively in this forest until 19 November 2006, when another wild population was released by the Department of Natural Resources in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Utuado" class="mw-redirect" title="Utuado">Utuado</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Abajo_State_Forest" title="R\xc3\xado Abajo State Forest">R\xc3\xado Abajo State Forest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>El Yunque National Forest contains one designated wilderness area, <a href="/wiki/El_Toro_Wilderness" title="El Toro Wilderness">El Toro Wilderness</a>, which is the only tropical rainforest in the United States <a href="/wiki/National_Wilderness_Preservation_System" title="National Wilderness Preservation System">National Wilderness Preservation System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ba\xc3\xb1o_de_Oro_Natural_Area"><span id="Ba.C3.B1o_de_Oro_Natural_Area"></span>Ba\xc3\xb1o de Oro Natural Area</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ba\xc3\xb1o de Oro Natural Area"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1237879389"><table class="infobox vcard"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above fn org">Ba\xc3\xb1o de Oro Natural Area</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-subheader" style="font-size: 100%;"><div style="background-color: #BFFFBF;"><a href="/wiki/IUCN_protected_area_categories" title="IUCN protected area categories">IUCN category</a> III (<a href="/wiki/Natural_monument" title="Natural monument">natural monument or feature</a>)</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Rain_Forest_of_El_Yunque,_Puerto_Rico.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Rain_Forest_of_El_Yunque%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg/284px-Rain_Forest_of_El_Yunque%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg" decoding="async" width="284" height="213" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Rain_Forest_of_El_Yunque%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg/426px-Rain_Forest_of_El_Yunque%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Rain_Forest_of_El_Yunque%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg/568px-Rain_Forest_of_El_Yunque%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></span></td></tr><tr class="locality"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Location</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Grande,_Puerto_Rico" title="R\xc3\xado Grande, Puerto Rico">R\xc3\xado Grande, Puerto Rico</a></td></tr><tr class="note"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Established</th><td class="infobox-data">1949</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1237879389"></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1237879389"><div style="border:4px solid #ACE1AF; line-height: 1.5; text-align: center;">\n<a href="/wiki/National_Natural_Landmark" title="National Natural Landmark">U.S. National Natural Landmark</a></div></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Designated</th><td class="infobox-data">1980</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr></tbody></table><p>The <b>Ba\xc3\xb1o de Oro Natural Area</b> is an 1,840-acre <a href="/wiki/Research_Natural_Area" title="Research Natural Area">Research Natural Area</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Natural_Landmark" title="National Natural Landmark">National Natural Landmark</a> located within El Yunque National Forest. The Research Natural Area was established by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">Forest Service</a> in 1949 with the goal of preserving a forest area situated between 800&#160;ft. and 3,365&#160;ft. in elevation encompassing two life zones, a rare <i><a href="/wiki/Pterocarpus" title="Pterocarpus">Pterocarpus</a></i> swamp, and four major forest types that are free of human disturbance for the purpose of scientific study, education and future conservation efforts. Ba\xc3\xb1o de Oro is listed as a National Natural Landmark since 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USDA_Ba\xc3\xb1o_de_Oro_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDA_Ba\xc3\xb1o_de_Oro-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Flora">Flora</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Flora"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><p>\nEl Yunque is composed of four different forest vegetation areas: <a href="/wiki/Dacryodes_excelsa" title="Dacryodes excelsa">Tabonuco</a> Forest, Palo Colorado Forest, <a href="/wiki/Prestoea_montana" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestoea montana">Sierra Palm</a> Forest, and the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_forest" title="Dwarf forest">Dwarf Forest</a>. El Yunque forest supports a vast array of animal and plant life that varies depending on the altitude range in the rainforest.</p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_El_Yunque_Rain_Forest.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/The_El_Yunque_Rain_Forest.jpg/220px-The_El_Yunque_Rain_Forest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/The_El_Yunque_Rain_Forest.jpg/330px-The_El_Yunque_Rain_Forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/The_El_Yunque_Rain_Forest.jpg/440px-The_El_Yunque_Rain_Forest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a><figcaption>Skyline of the Sierra palm tree forest in El Yunque National Forest from a trail.</figcaption></figure>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sierra_palm_tree_forest">Sierra palm tree forest</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sierra palm tree forest"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Seeds_of_the_sierra_palm.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Seeds_of_the_sierra_palm.jpg/220px-Seeds_of_the_sierra_palm.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Seeds_of_the_sierra_palm.jpg/330px-Seeds_of_the_sierra_palm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Seeds_of_the_sierra_palm.jpg/440px-Seeds_of_the_sierra_palm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>The seeds of the <a href="/wiki/Prestoea_acuminata_var._montana" title="Prestoea acuminata var. montana">Sierra palm</a> </figcaption></figure>\n<p>This forest area is located at 1,970 feet (600 m) above sea level and is dominated by the Sierra palm tree (<i><a href="/wiki/Prestoea_montana" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestoea montana">Prestoea montana</a></i>). This palm tree can be found throughout the national forest but the shallowness of the soil makes it the dominant tree at this height of the forest.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Palo_colorado_forest">Palo colorado forest</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Palo colorado forest"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>This forest area is found above 2,500 feet (600\xe2\x80\x93900 m) above sea level and is dominated by the <i>palo colorado</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Cyrilla_racemiflora" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyrilla racemiflora">Cyrilla racemiflora</a></i>). The most distinctive features of this tree are its red crooked bark which gives it its Spanish name. These trees are not endemic to the island but they are native to the region. This forest area is a partial <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">old growth forest</a> and some of the <i>palo colorado</i> trees are estimated to be up to 1,000 years old. Due to the shallowness of its bark, the trunks of these trees are commonly used as nesting sites by <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> Puerto Rican parrots (<i><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_amazon" title="Puerto Rican amazon">Amazona vittata</a></i>).\n</p><p>Other trees found in the <i>palo colorado</i> forest area <i>caimitillo</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Micropholis_garciniifolia" title="Micropholis garciniifolia">Micropholis garcinifolia</a></i>), green <i>caimitillo</i> (<i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Micropholis_garciniaefolia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Micropholis garciniaefolia (page does not exist)">Micropholis garciniaefolia</a></i>), yarumo (<i><a href="/wiki/Cecropia_peltata" title="Cecropia peltata">Cecropia peltata</a></i>), Caribbean <i>azafran</i> (<i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hedyosmum_arborencens&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hedyosmum arborencens (page does not exist)">Hedyosmum arborencens</a></i>) and the Sierra palm tree (<i><a href="/wiki/Prestoea_montana" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestoea montana">Prestoea montana</a></i>).\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tabonuco_forest">Tabonuco forest</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tabonuco forest"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tabonuco" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabonuco">tabonuco</a> tree (<i><a href="/wiki/Dacryodes_excelsa" title="Dacryodes excelsa">Dacryodes excelsa</a></i>) from which this forest area got its name occurs from 660 to 2800 feet (200 to 900 meters) in the mountains of Puerto Rico as well as other islands that make up the <a href="/wiki/Antilles" title="Antilles">Antilles</a>. The laurel magnolia (<i><a href="/wiki/Magnolia_splendens" title="Magnolia splendens">Magnolia splendens</a></i>), an <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> magnolia tree that is <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> to eastern Puerto Rico, can be found in this forest area as well. This forest is very diverse in trees, having over 170 species such as bulletwood (<i><a href="/wiki/Manilkara_bidentata" title="Manilkara bidentata">Manilkara bidentata</a></i>), West Indian giant fern (<i><a href="/wiki/Cyathea_arborea" title="Cyathea arborea">Cyathea arborea</a></i>), yarumo (<i><a href="/wiki/Cecropia_peltata" title="Cecropia peltata">Cecropia peltata</a></i>), macho yarumo (<i><a href="/wiki/Schefflera_morototoni" class="mw-redirect" title="Schefflera morototoni">Didymopanax morototoni</a></i>), granadillo (<i><a href="/wiki/Buchenavia_capitata" title="Buchenavia capitata">Buchenavia capitata</a></i>) and guaraguao tree (<i><a href="/wiki/Guarea_guidonia" title="Guarea guidonia">Guarea guidona</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dwarf_forest_(cloud_forest)"><span id="Dwarf_forest_.28cloud_forest.29"></span>Dwarf forest (cloud forest)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dwarf forest (cloud forest)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_forest" title="Dwarf forest">dwarf forest</a> ecosystem is located at around 3,000 feet (910&#160;m) and composes the smallest sub-region in El Yunque forest. The forest is characterized by the variation of vegetation that is only found in Puerto Rico. The vegetation shows stunted growth in which the diameter of the trunk is widened and the number of leaves on the branches is lower than expected.<sup id="cite_ref-Weaver_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other specific factors that affect the growth of this sub-region are the high level of acidity and poor water runoff from the soil.\n</p><p>Although many species have adapted to these harsh environments, five species are frequent in the dwarf forest: <i><a href="/wiki/Ocotea" title="Ocotea">Ocotea spathulata</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tabebuia" title="Tabebuia">Tabebuia rigida</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Calyptranthes" class="mw-redirect" title="Calyptranthes">Calyptranthes krugii</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_Eugenia_species" title="List of Eugenia species">Eugenia borinquensis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Calycogonium" class="mw-redirect" title="Calycogonium">Calycogonium squamulosum</a></i>. The other abundant type of plants in the dwarf forest are <a href="/wiki/Epiphyte" title="Epiphyte">epiphytes</a>. The great amount of <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">competition</a> in the canopy does not allow lower-level plants to develop and prosper.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The characteristic of having a widened tree trunk is ideal for epiphytes that require a host to live. Therefore, a substantial number of epiphytic plants have cemented their existence in the flora of El Yunque forest, specifically in the dwarf forest due to the moisture, precipitation and protection from the sun.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Fauna">Fauna</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fauna"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Amazona_vitatta_-Iguaca_Aviary,_Puerto_Rico-8a.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amazona_vitatta_-Iguaca_Aviary%2C_Puerto_Rico-8a.jpg/220px-Amazona_vitatta_-Iguaca_Aviary%2C_Puerto_Rico-8a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amazona_vitatta_-Iguaca_Aviary%2C_Puerto_Rico-8a.jpg/330px-Amazona_vitatta_-Iguaca_Aviary%2C_Puerto_Rico-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/Amazona_vitatta_-Iguaca_Aviary%2C_Puerto_Rico-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="251" /></a><figcaption>Puerto Rican parrots at the aviary in El Yunque</figcaption></figure>\n<p>The national forest is home to numerous species of animals, many of which are endemic to Puerto Rico. In addition to the critically endangered Puerto Rican parrot, other endangered species found in the forest are the Puerto Rican broad-winged hawk (<i><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_broad-winged_hawk" title="Puerto Rican broad-winged hawk">Buteo platypterus brunnescens</a></i>), the Puerto Rican sharp-shinned hawk (<i><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_sharp-shinned_hawk" title="Puerto Rican sharp-shinned hawk">Accipiter striatus venator</a></i>), the Elfin woods warbler (<i><a href="/wiki/Elfin_woods_warbler" title="Elfin woods warbler">Setophaga angelae</a></i>) and the Puerto Rican boa (<i><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_boa" title="Puerto Rican boa">Epicratus inornatus</a></i>). The white-necked crow (<i><a href="/wiki/White-necked_crow" title="White-necked crow">Corvus leucognaphalus</a></i>), which used to be found in the region, has been completely extirpated from the island and is now only found in <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>.\n</p><p>Other animals found within the forest boundaries are the Puerto Rican oriole (<a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_oriole" title="Puerto Rican oriole"><i>Icterus portoricensis</i></a>), the peregrine falcon (<i><a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">Falco peregrinus</a></i>), red fruit bat (<i><a href="/wiki/Red_fruit_bat" title="Red fruit bat">Stenoderma rufus</a></i>), the Puerto Rican giant anole (<i><a href="/wiki/Anolis_cuvieri" title="Anolis cuvieri">Anolis cuvieri</a></i>) and the Puerto Rican twig anole (<i><a href="/wiki/Anolis_occultus" title="Anolis occultus">Anolis occultus</a></i>). The endangered American eel (<a href="/wiki/American_eel" title="American eel"><i>Anguilla rostrata</i></a>), the fat sleeper fish (<i><a href="/wiki/Dormitator_maculatus" title="Dormitator maculatus">Dormitator maculatus</a></i>), the bigmouth sleeper fish (<i><a href="/wiki/Gobiomorus" title="Gobiomorus">Gobiomorus dormitor</a></i>), big claw river shrimp (<i><a href="/wiki/Macrobrachium_carcinus" title="Macrobrachium carcinus">Macrobrachium carcinus</a></i>) and the boc\xc3\xba shrimp (<i><a href="/wiki/Macrobrachium_crenulatum" title="Macrobrachium crenulatum">Macrobrachium crenulatum</a></i>) are found in the rivers and creeks that originate and flow from El Yunque.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Puerto_Rican_Amazon">Puerto Rican Amazon</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Puerto Rican Amazon"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_amazon" title="Puerto Rican amazon">Puerto Rican parrot</a> or Puerto Rican Amazon is a little parrot that measures 11.0\xe2\x80\x9311.8&#160;in (28\xe2\x80\x9330&#160;cm). The bird is a predominantly green parrot with a red forehead and white rings around the eyes. The species is the only remaining native parrot in Puerto Rico. The total estimated population as of 2012 was 58\xe2\x80\x9380 individuals in the wild and over 300 individuals in captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Coqui">Coqui</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Coqui"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coqu%C3%AD" title="Coqu\xc3\xad">Coqu\xc3\xad</a></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg/220px-Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="210" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="287" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Coqu%C3%AD" title="Coqu\xc3\xad">coqu\xc3\xad</a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>Approximately 16 species of common coqui, members of the diverse neotropical frog genus <i><a href="/wiki/Eleutherodactylus" title="Eleutherodactylus">Eleutherodactylus</a></i>, are known in Puerto Rico. Of these 16, 13 have been found in El Yunque National Forest.<sup id="cite_ref-Burrowes_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burrowes-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This small frog earned its Puerto Rican common name due to the call of the most common coqu\xc3\xad species in Puerto Rico, <i><a href="/wiki/Eleutherodactylus_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleutherodactylus coqui">Eleutherodactylus coqui</a></i>, which begins as the sun sets and ends in early dawn. This has made it an animal of great endearment to Puerto Ricans and in contemporary times the coqui has become a symbol of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Ricans" title="Puerto Ricans">Puerto Ricans</a>.\n</p><p>Although the coqu\xc3\xad is an amphibian, it possesses some features that are unusual in frogs. These differences are seen mainly in its morphology, reproduction, and developmental stages. In terms of morphology, the coqu\xc3\xad does not have webbing between its toes because it is a tree dweller in moist environments. Another significant difference is that it does not have a definite larval stage and the eggs laid by the female are terrestrial instead of aquatic.<sup id="cite_ref-Burrowes_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burrowes-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This means that a miniature frog-let, rather than a tadpole, arises from the incubation period.\n</p><p>Other species of coqui found in the forest are the grass coqui (<i><a href="/wiki/Grass_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Grass coqui">Eleutherodactylus brittoni</a></i>), Eneida\'s coqui (<i><a href="/wiki/Eneida%27s_coqu%C3%AD" title="Eneida&#39;s coqu\xc3\xad">Eleutherodactylus eneidae</a></i>), the cricket coqui (<i><a href="/wiki/Cricket_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Cricket coqui">Eleutherodactylus gryllus</a></i>), Hedrick\'s coqui (<a href="/wiki/Hedrick%27s_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Hedrick&#39;s coqui"><i>Eleutherodactylus hedricki</i></a>), the web-footed coqui (<a href="/wiki/Web-footed_coqu%C3%AD" title="Web-footed coqu\xc3\xad"><i>Eleutherodactylus karlschmidti</i></a>), the locust coqui (<a href="/wiki/Locust_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Locust coqui"><i>Eleutherodactylus locustus</i></a>), the upland or forest coqui (<i><a href="/wiki/Eleutherodactylus_portoricensis" title="Eleutherodactylus portoricensis">Eleutherodactylus portoricensis</a></i>), the bronze or Richmond\'s coqui (<a href="/wiki/Bronze_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze coqui"><i>Eleutherodactylus richmondi</i></a>), the dwarf coqui (<a href="/wiki/Dwarf_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwarf coqui"><i>Eleutherodactylus unicolor</i></a>) and the melodious or wrinkled coqui (<a href="/wiki/Melodius_coqui" class="mw-redirect" title="Melodius coqui"><i>Eleutherodactylus wightmanae</i></a>).\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Arthropods">Arthropods</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Arthropods"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change" title="Effects of climate change">Effects of climate change</a></div>\n<p>Per a study published October 2018, by Bradford C. Lister and Andres Garcia, arthropod <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> in the Luquillo rainforest data taken during the <a href="/wiki/1970s" title="1970s">1970s</a> compared to 30 years later has fallen 10 to 60 times. The study revealed synchronous declines in the lizards, frogs, and birds that eat arthropods. The study indicated that climate warming is the driving force behind the collapse of the forest\'s food web. Over the past 30 years, forest temperatures have risen 2.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Recreation">Recreation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Recreation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Yokahu_Tower,_El_Yunque_National_Forest.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Yokahu_Tower%2C_El_Yunque_National_Forest.jpg/220px-Yokahu_Tower%2C_El_Yunque_National_Forest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Yokahu_Tower%2C_El_Yunque_National_Forest.jpg/330px-Yokahu_Tower%2C_El_Yunque_National_Forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Yokahu_Tower%2C_El_Yunque_National_Forest.jpg/440px-Yokahu_Tower%2C_El_Yunque_National_Forest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="6000" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Yokah%C3%BA_Tower" title="Yokah\xc3\xba Tower">Yokahu Tower</a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>In addition to being an important ecological forest reserve, El Yunque National Forest is one of the most popular tourist attractions in Puerto Rico,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> both for locals and visitors, and it offers numerous recreational opportunities such as picnicking, birdwatching, and biking in designated areas. Camping is allowed in designated areas (depending on the size of the group) and there are also cabins available for rent. Additionally, the forest is known for its vista points and observation towers: <a href="/wiki/Yokah%C3%BA_Tower" title="Yokah\xc3\xba Tower">Yokahu Tower</a> and Mount Britton Tower.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Hiking_Trails">Hiking Trails</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hiking Trails"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:El_Yunque_Trails_Map.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/El_Yunque_Trails_Map.png/220px-El_Yunque_Trails_Map.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="323" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/El_Yunque_Trails_Map.png/330px-El_Yunque_Trails_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/El_Yunque_Trails_Map.png/440px-El_Yunque_Trails_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="585" data-file-height="860" /></a><figcaption>A map of hiking trails and landmarks along <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Highway_191" title="Puerto Rico Highway 191">PR-191</a> in El Yunque National Forest</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Hiking is probably the most popular activity in El Yunque National Forest.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The forest offers several trails with varying difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The 35.9 mile (57.8&#160;km) long <a href="/w/index.php?title=Puerto_Rican_Northeast_Trail&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Puerto Rican Northeast Trail (page does not exist)">Puerto Rican Northeast Trail</a> also connects to and traverses through El Yunque.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Pico_El_Yunque">Pico El Yunque</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pico El Yunque"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Pico El Yunque Trail (or just El Yunque Trail) is the main hiking trail (or at least the most popular)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> of the forest and it extends for about 5.4 miles that lead to the summit of <a href="/wiki/El_Yunque_(Puerto_Rico)" title="El Yunque (Puerto Rico)">El Yunque</a>, the second highest point of the Sierra de Luquillo. Although considered moderate in difficulty, some of its side trails are considered to be more challenging; the trail itself has an elevation gain of 1,748 feet. The trailhead is located close to the end of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Highway_191" title="Puerto Rico Highway 191">PR-191</a> and La Mina Falls, next to the <a href="/wiki/Ternstroemia_luquillensis" title="Ternstroemia luquillensis">Palo Colorado</a> Information Center, in an area of the national forest often referred to as the La Mina Recreational Area. The trail crosses all four biomes of the national forest. It is open year-round and dogs are also allowed on this trail but must be kept on a leash at all times.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Mount_Britton_Tower">Mount Britton Tower</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mount Britton Tower"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Mt. Britton Tower Trail is a small 1.6-mile trail that starts off at El Yunque Trail and crosses a <a href="/wiki/Prestoea_montana" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestoea montana">Sierra palm</a> forest on the way to the top of Mount Britton, the 8th tallest peak in the forest. The summit is part of the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_forest" title="Cloud forest">cloud forest</a> and it hosts a small lookout tower built by the <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a> in 1937 that on clear days offers views of both the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The trail has an elevation gain of approximately 650 feet and although it is rated as moderate to difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Los_Picachos"><a href="/wiki/Los_Picachos" title="Los Picachos">Los Picachos</a></h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Los Picachos"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The very short but steep trail starts off Pico El Yunque Trail and it extends for little more than 0.2 miles to the top, where a stone masonry platform offers a 360 panoramic view of the surrounding mountains and the eastern coast of Puerto Rico. The vegetation along this trail belongs to that of the rare <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_forest" title="Dwarf forest">dwarf forest</a> ecosystem which features "dwarf trees" such as the miniature tree <i><a href="/wiki/Clusia_clusioides" title="Clusia clusioides">Clusia clusioides</a></i>, and equally small species of animals such as the dwarf anole (<i><a href="/wiki/Anolis_occultus" title="Anolis occultus">Anolis occultus</a></i>) and the endangered elfin woods warbler (<a href="/wiki/Elfin_woods_warbler" title="Elfin woods warbler"><i>Setophaga angelae</i></a>). This ecological zone is also known as the elfin woodlands.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although very short, the trail difficulty is classified as moderate to difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Caimitillo">Caimitillo</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Caimitillo"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>This is a short and easy 0.4-mile trail that begins close to the Sierra Palm Visitor Center on PR-191. The trail crosses a rainy forest primarily inhabited by tree ferns (<i><a href="/wiki/Cyathea_arborea" title="Cyathea arborea">Cyathea arborea</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Alsophila_dryopteroides" title="Alsophila dryopteroides">Alsophila dryopteroides</a></i>) and Sierra palm trees. The trail leads to the <a href="/wiki/Micropholis_garciniifolia" title="Micropholis garciniifolia">Caimitillo</a> picnic area. Dogs are also allowed on this trail but must be kept on a leash at all times.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Angelito">Angelito</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Angelito"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The Angelito Trail is an easy short 0.7-mile-long trail that crosses the <a href="/wiki/Dacryodes_excelsa" title="Dacryodes excelsa">tabonuco</a> forest zone leads to the Las Damas Pool, also known as Charco Angelito, a series of natural pools along the <a href="/wiki/Mameyes_River" title="Mameyes River">Mameyes River</a>.  Although the trail is easy visitors must be cautious of the weather since the area is prone to <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash flooding</a>. The trail and area were heavily damaged during <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Maria" title="Hurricane Maria">Hurricane Maria</a> in 2017 but it has recently reopened. Dogs are allowed on the trail but must be kept on a leash. The trailhead is located on PR-988. This trail is the only one in the forest to be located in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Luquillo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Luquillo, Puerto Rico">Luquillo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="La_Coca">La Coca</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: La Coca"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Yunque_waterfall.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Yunque_waterfall.jpg/170px-Yunque_waterfall.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Yunque_waterfall.jpg/255px-Yunque_waterfall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Yunque_waterfall.jpg/340px-Yunque_waterfall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a><figcaption>Coca Falls</figcaption></figure>\n<p>This trail is a 1.8 miles one-way trail that is considered to be very challenging, not necessarily for its steepness but for the denseness of the forest foliage it crosses. The trail crosses numerous small steams, waterfalls and a <a href="/wiki/Dacryodes_excelsa" title="Dacryodes excelsa">tabonuco</a> tree forest and leads to the lush <a href="/wiki/Mameyes_River" title="Mameyes River">Mameyes</a> <a href="/wiki/National_Wild_and_Scenic_Rivers_System" title="National Wild and Scenic Rivers System">Wild &amp; Scenic River</a> area. This is one of the rainiest parts of the national forest which means the trail is often muddy and slippery so appropriate hiking shoes are necessary. Due to the dense forest in the area, most disappearances in the forest occur along this trail so it is important not to walk off the trail under any circumstance. The trailhead is located on <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Highway_191" title="Puerto Rico Highway 191">PR-191</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="La_Mina">La Mina</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: La Mina"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hike_to_La_Mina_Falls_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest,_Puerto_Rico.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Hike_to_La_Mina_Falls_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg/220px-Hike_to_La_Mina_Falls_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Hike_to_La_Mina_Falls_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg/330px-Hike_to_La_Mina_Falls_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Hike_to_La_Mina_Falls_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg/440px-Hike_to_La_Mina_Falls_in_El_Yunque_National_Forest%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>Hike to La Mina Falls</figcaption></figure>\n<p>La Mina Trail is a moderate to difficult 0.7-mile-long mile that starts on PR-191 close to the Palo Colorado Information Center. This is another popular hiking trail as it leads to <a href="/w/index.php?title=La_Mina_Falls&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="La Mina Falls (page does not exist)">La Mina Falls</a>, one of the most picturesque waterfalls of the national forest. The trail also follows the course of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_de_la_Mina_(R%C3%ADo_Grande,_Puerto_Rico)" title="R\xc3\xado de la Mina (R\xc3\xado Grande, Puerto Rico)">La Mina River</a>, also included in the <a href="/wiki/National_Wild_and_Scenic_Rivers_System" title="National Wild and Scenic Rivers System">National Wild and Scenic Rivers System</a>, and offers opportunities for swimming in the pool located by La Mina Falls. The trail is closed as of 2021 due to the damage caused by Hurricane Maria in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Big_Tree">Big Tree</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Big Tree"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>This is a paved mile-long self-guided interpretative trail that offers hikers the opportunity to come close to some of the largest trees in the national forest, the tabonuco tree (Dacryodes excelsa). Although paved the trail is considered moderate as it is steep in some sections. It ends in La Mina Falls close to a picnic area. The trail is closed as of 2021 due to the damage caused by Hurricane Maria in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="R\xc3\xado_Sabana"><span id="R.C3.ADo_Sabana"></span>R\xc3\xado Sabana</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: R\xc3\xado Sabana"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>This trail is one of the few to be located in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Naguabo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Naguabo, Puerto Rico">Naguabo</a>, in the southern portion of the national forest. The trailhead is located on the southern section of PR-191 and it hosts bathrooms and a picnic area. The trail was originally built by the Civilian Conservation Corps in the 1930s and remained close for many decades but it was finally reopened in 2011 as part of the Sabana Recreation Area. This trail is considered difficult as it crosses dense forest and remote steep areas; it extends 2.2 miles and it connects to the El Toro and Tradewinds Trails in <a href="/wiki/El_Toro_Wilderness" title="El Toro Wilderness">El Toro National Wilderness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="El_Toro,_Tradewinds"><span id="El_Toro.2C_Tradewinds"></span>El Toro, Tradewinds</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: El Toro, Tradewinds"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/El_Toro_Wilderness" title="El Toro Wilderness">El Toro Wilderness</a> Trail is one of the most remote and challenging trails in El Yunque National Forest. It extends for 2.9 miles on its paved section where it connects to the R\xc3\xado Sabana and the Tradewinds trails. When including the unpaved section (also known as the Tradewinds Trail), the trail extends for almost 5 miles and it leads to <a href="/wiki/El_Toro_(Sierra_de_Luquillo)" title="El Toro (Sierra de Luquillo)">El Toro</a>, which at 3,526 feet (1,075 m) is the highest point in both the national forest and eastern Puerto Rico. The trail crosses all four forest biomes through the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/Can%C3%B3vanas,_Puerto_Rico" title="Can\xc3\xb3vanas, Puerto Rico">Can\xc3\xb3vanas</a>, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Grande,_Puerto_Rico" title="R\xc3\xado Grande, Puerto Rico">R\xc3\xado Grande</a>, <a href="/wiki/Las_Piedras,_Puerto_Rico" title="Las Piedras, Puerto Rico">Las Piedras</a> (at El Toro\'s summit) and <a href="/wiki/Naguabo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Naguabo, Puerto Rico">Naguabo</a>, fully within El Toro National Wilderness. This part of the national forest is the best ecologically preserved and it is home to numerous endemic and endangered species such as the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_amazon" title="Puerto Rican amazon">Puerto Rican parrot</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_boa" title="Puerto Rican boa">Puerto Rican boa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Elfin_woods_warbler" title="Elfin woods warbler">elfin woods warbler</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red_fruit_bat" title="Red fruit bat">red fruit bat</a>, and at least five species of <a href="/wiki/Coqu%C3%AD" title="Coqu\xc3\xad">coquis</a>. The trailhead is located on <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Highway_186" title="Puerto Rico Highway 186">PR-186</a> in a section of the road known as the El Toro Scenic Byway, in <a href="/wiki/Cubuy,_Can%C3%B3vanas,_Puerto_Rico" title="Cubuy, Can\xc3\xb3vanas, Puerto Rico">Cubuy, Can\xc3\xb3vanas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Both the El Toro and Tradewinds trails are listed as <a href="/wiki/National_Recreation_Trail" class="mw-redirect" title="National Recreation Trail">National Recreation Trails</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nrt1_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrt1-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrt2_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrt2-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Swimming">Swimming</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Yunque_National_Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Swimming"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Kids_at_play_at_El_Yunque_rainforest_in_Puerto_Rico_in_2007.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Kids_at_play_at_El_Yunque_rainforest_in_Puerto_Rico_in_2007.jpg/220px-Kids_at_play_at_El_Yunque_rainforest_in_Puerto_Rico_in_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Kids_at_play_at_El_Yunque_rainforest_in_Puerto_Rico_in_2007.jpg/330px-Kids_at_play_at_El_Yunque_rainforest_in_Puerto_Rico_in_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Kids_at_play_at_El_Yunque_rainforest_in_Puerto_Rico_in_2007.jpg/440px-Kids_at_play_at_El_Yunque_rainforest_in_Puerto_Rico_in_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3058" data-file-height="2050" /></a><figcaption>Kids at play at El Yunque rainforest in 2007</figcaption></figure>\n<p>El Yunque National Forest offers many opportunities for swimming in natural pools often found at the base of waterfalls, such as that of La Mina Falls and the Juan Diego area off PR-191. Some areas available for swimming are the Quebrada Grande Recreation Area and the Esp\xc3\xadritu Santo Observation Point off PR-186, the Sabana Recreation Area off the southern section of PR-191 in Naguabo, and the Angelito and Puente Roto Recreation Areas off PR-988 in Luquillo.
